<p><b>90377 Sedna</b> is a large <a href="/wiki/Minor_planet" title="Minor planet">minor planet</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">outer reaches of the Solar System</a> that was, as of 2015<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, at a distance of about 86 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">astronomical units</a> (AU) from the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a>, about three times as far as <a href="/wiki/Neptune" title="Neptune">Neptune</a>. <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">Spectroscopy</a> has revealed that Sedna's surface composition is similar to that of some other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_objects" title="Trans-Neptunian objects">trans-Neptunian objects</a>, being largely a mixture of water, <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Solid_nitrogen" title="Solid nitrogen">nitrogen</a> <a href="/wiki/Volatiles" title="Volatiles">ices</a> with <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>. Its surface is one of the <a href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_object#Colors" title="Trans-Neptunian object">reddest</a> among Solar System objects. It is most <a href="/wiki/List_of_possible_dwarf_planets" title="List of possible dwarf planets">likely a dwarf planet</a>.</p>
<p>For most of its orbit, it is even farther from the Sun than at present, with its <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">aphelion</a> estimated at 937 AU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barycenter_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-barycenter-3">[3]</a></sup> (31 times Neptune's distance), making it one of the most distant known objects in the <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">Solar System</a> other than <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Long-period_comet" title="Long-period comet">long-period comets</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[b]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteF_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteF-14">[c]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sedna has an exceptionally long and elongated orbit, taking approximately 11,400 years to complete and a distant point of closest approach to the Sun at 76 AU. These facts have led to much speculation about its origin. The <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a> currently places Sedna in the <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a>, a group of objects sent into highly elongated orbits by the gravitational influence of Neptune. However, this classification has been contested, because Sedna never comes close enough to Neptune to have been scattered by it, leading some astronomers to conclude that it is in fact the first known member of the <a href="/wiki/Hills_cloud" title="Hills cloud">inner Oort cloud</a>. Others speculate that it might have been tugged into its current orbit by a passing star, perhaps one within the Sun's birth cluster (an <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">open cluster</a>), or even that it was captured from another star system. Another hypothesis suggests that its orbit may be evidence for a large <a href="/wiki/Planets_beyond_Neptune" title="Planets beyond Neptune">planet beyond the orbit of Neptune</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mike-15">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Astronomer <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Michael E. Brown</a>, co-discoverer of Sedna and the dwarf planets <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, thinks that it is the most scientifically important trans-Neptunian object found to date, because understanding its unusual orbit is likely to yield valuable information about the origin and early evolution of the Solar System.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fussman_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-fussman-16">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Discovery">Discovery</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Discovery">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sedna (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Provisional_designation" title="Provisional designation">provisionally designated</a> <b><span class="nowrap">2003 VB<sub>12</sub></span></b>) was discovered by <a href="/wiki/Michael_E._Brown" title="Michael E. Brown">Michael Brown</a> (<a href="/wiki/California_Institute_of_Technology" title="California Institute of Technology">Caltech</a>), <a href="/wiki/Chad_Trujillo" title="Chad Trujillo">Chad Trujillo</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gemini_Observatory" title="Gemini Observatory">Gemini Observatory</a>), and <a href="/wiki/David_L._Rabinowitz" title="David L. Rabinowitz">David Rabinowitz</a> (<a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale University</a>) on 14 November 2003. The discovery formed part of a survey begun in 2001 with the <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Oschin_telescope" title="Samuel Oschin telescope">Samuel Oschin telescope</a> at <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Observatory" title="Palomar Observatory">Palomar Observatory</a> near <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/San_Diego,_California" title="San Diego, California">San Diego</a>, <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a> using Yale's 160 megapixel <a href="/wiki/Palomar_Observatory#QUEST" title="Palomar Observatory">Palomar Quest camera</a>. On that day, an object was observed to move by 4.6 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minute_of_arc" title="Minute of arc">arcseconds</a> over 3.1 hours relative to stars, which indicated that its distance was about 100 AU. Follow-up observations in November–December 2003 with the SMARTS telescope at <a href="/wiki/Cerro_Tololo_Inter-American_Observatory" title="Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory">Cerro Tololo Inter-American Observatory</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a> as well as with the Tenagra IV telescope at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Keck_Observatory" title="Keck Observatory">Keck Observatory</a> on <a href="/wiki/Mauna_Kea" title="Mauna Kea">Mauna Kea</a> in Hawaii revealed that the object was moving along a distant highly <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a> orbit. Later, the object was <a href="/wiki/Precovery" title="Precovery">precovered</a> on older images made by the Samuel Oschin telescope as well as on images from the <a href="/wiki/Near-Earth_Asteroid_Tracking" title="Near-Earth Asteroid Tracking">Near-Earth Asteroid Tracking</a> consortium. These previous positions expanded its known orbital arc and allowed a more precise calculation of its orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mike-15">[12]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Naming">Naming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Naming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>"Our newly discovered object is the coldest most distant place known in the Solar System", said Mike Brown on his website, "so we feel it is appropriate to name it in honor of <a href="/wiki/Sedna_(mythology)" title="Sedna (mythology)">Sedna</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inuit_mythology" title="Inuit mythology">Inuit goddess</a> of the sea, who is thought to live at the bottom of the frigid <a href="/wiki/Arctic_Ocean" title="Arctic Ocean">Arctic Ocean</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mikebrown_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-mikebrown-18">[15]</a></sup> Brown also suggested to the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a>'s (IAU) Minor Planet Center that any future objects discovered in Sedna's orbital region should also be named after entities in arctic mythologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mikebrown_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-mikebrown-18">[15]</a></sup> The team made the name "Sedna" public before the object had been officially numbered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpc_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-mpc-19">[16]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Brian_G._Marsden" title="Brian G. Marsden">Brian Marsden</a>, the head of the Minor Planet Center, said that such an action was a violation of protocol, and that some members of the IAU might vote against it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walker_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walker-20">[17]</a></sup> However, no objection was raised to the name, and no competing names were suggested. The IAU's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Committee_on_Small_Body_Nomenclature" title="Committee on Small Body Nomenclature">Committee on Small Body Nomenclature</a> formally accepted the name in September 2004,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPC_20040928_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPC_20040928-21">[18]</a></sup> and also considered that, in similar cases of extraordinary interest, it might in the future allow names to be announced before they were officially numbered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mpc_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-mpc-19">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orbit_and_rotation">Orbit and rotation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Orbit and rotation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sedna_orbit.svg"><img alt="The orbit of Sedna lies well beyond these objects, and extends many times their distances from the Sun" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="450" data-file-width="1150" height="86" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Sedna_orbit.svg/220px-Sedna_orbit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Sedna_orbit.svg/330px-Sedna_orbit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/Sedna_orbit.svg/440px-Sedna_orbit.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sedna_orbit.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The orbit of Sedna (red) set against the orbits of outer Solar System objects (Pluto's orbit is purple, Neptune's is blue).</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sedna has the longest <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">orbital period</a> of any known object in the Solar System of comparable size or larger,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteF_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteF-14">[c]</a></sup> calculated at around 11,400 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barycenter_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-barycenter-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteG_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteG-6">[a]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Orbit" title="Orbit">orbit</a> is extremely <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">eccentric</a>, with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aphelion" title="Aphelion">aphelion</a> estimated at 937 AU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barycenter_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-barycenter-3">[3]</a></sup> and a <a href="/wiki/Perihelion_and_aphelion" title="Perihelion and aphelion">perihelion</a> at about 76 AU. This perihelion was the largest of that of any known Solar System object until the discovery of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2012_VP113" title="2012 VP113">2012 VP<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">113</span></span></a></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo2007_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo2007-22">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo2014_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo2014-23">[20]</a></sup> When Sedna was discovered it was 89.6 AU<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys2003_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys2003-24">[21]</a></sup> from the Sun approaching perihelion, and was the most distant object in the Solar System observed. <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a> was later detected by the same survey near aphelion at 97 AU. Only the orbits of some long-period comets extend farther than that of Sedna; they are too dim to be discovered except when approaching perihelion in the inner Solar System. Even as Sedna nears its perihelion in mid 2076,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons2076_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons2076-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteC_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteC-25">[d]</a></sup> the Sun would appear merely as an extremely bright star-like pinpoint in its sky, 100 times brighter than a full moon on Earth (for comparison, the Sun appears from Earth to be roughly 400,000 times brighter than the full Moon), and too far away to be visible as a disc to the naked eye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HubbleSite2004image_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-HubbleSite2004image-26">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>When first discovered, Sedna was thought to have an unusually long rotational period (20 to 50 days).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HubbleSite2004image_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-HubbleSite2004image-26">[22]</a></sup> It was initially speculated that Sedna's rotation was slowed by the gravitational pull of a large binary companion, similar to <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>'s moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mikebrown_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-mikebrown-18">[15]</a></sup> A search for such a satellite by the <a href="/wiki/Hubble_Space_Telescope" title="Hubble Space Telescope">Hubble Space Telescope</a> in March 2004 found nothing,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HubbleSite2004_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-HubbleSite2004-27">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[e]</a></sup> and subsequent measurements from the <a href="/wiki/MMT_Observatory" title="MMT Observatory">MMT</a> telescope suggest a much shorter rotation period of about 10 hours, more typical for a body of its size.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gaudi2005_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gaudi2005-30">[25]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable sortable" style="text-align: center;">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Physical_characteristics">Physical characteristics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Physical characteristics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sedna has a V-band <a href="/wiki/Absolute_magnitude#Solar_System_bodies_.28H.29" title="Absolute magnitude">absolute magnitude</a> (H) of about 1.8, and it is estimated to have an <a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a> of about 0.32, thus giving it a diameter of approximately 1,000 km.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-herschel_7-3"><a href="#cite_note-herschel-7">[6]</a></sup> At the time of its discovery it was the intrinsically brightest object found in the Solar System since Pluto in 1930. In 2004, the discoverers placed an upper limit of 1,800 km on its diameter,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Grundy2005_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Grundy2005-32">[27]</a></sup> but by 2007 this was revised downward to less than 1,600 km after observation by the <a href="/wiki/Spitzer_Space_Telescope" title="Spitzer Space Telescope">Spitzer Space Telescope</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-spitzer_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-spitzer-33">[28]</a></sup> In 2012, measurements from the <a href="/wiki/Herschel_Space_Observatory" title="Herschel Space Observatory">Herschel Space Observatory</a> suggested that Sedna's diameter was <span class="nowrap">995 ± 80 km</span>, which would make it smaller than Pluto's moon <a href="/wiki/Charon_(moon)" title="Charon (moon)">Charon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-herschel_7-4"><a href="#cite_note-herschel-7">[6]</a></sup> Because Sedna has no known moons, determining its mass is currently impossible without sending a <a href="/wiki/Space_probe" title="Space probe">space probe</a>. Sedna is currently the largest trans-Neptunian Sun-orbiting object not known to possess a satellite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lakdawalla2016a_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lakdawalla2016a-34">[29]</a></sup></p>
<p>Observations from the SMARTS telescope show that in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Visible_light" title="Visible light">visible light</a> Sedna is one of the reddest objects in the Solar System, nearly as red as <a href="/wiki/Mars" title="Mars">Mars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mikebrown_18-3"><a href="#cite_note-mikebrown-18">[15]</a></sup> Chad Trujillo and his colleagues suggest that Sedna's dark red colour is caused by a surface coating of <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbon</a> sludge, or <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholin</a>, formed from simpler organic compounds after long exposure to <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> radiation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo2005_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo2005-35">[30]</a></sup> Its surface is homogeneous in colour and <a href="/wiki/Spectrograph" title="Spectrograph">spectrum</a>; this may be because Sedna, unlike objects nearer the Sun, is rarely impacted by other bodies, which would expose bright patches of fresh icy material like that on <a href="/wiki/8405_Asbolus" title="8405 Asbolus">8405 Asbolus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo2005_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo2005-35">[30]</a></sup> Sedna and two other very distant objects – <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2006_SQ372" title="2006 SQ372">2006 SQ<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">372</span></span></a></span> and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(87269)_2000_OO67" title="(87269) 2000 OO67">(87269) 2000 OO<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">67</span></span></a></span> – share their color with outer <a href="/wiki/Classical_Kuiper_belt_object" title="Classical Kuiper belt object">classical Kuiper belt objects</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Centaur_(planetoid)" title="Centaur (planetoid)">centaur</a> <a href="/wiki/5145_Pholus" title="5145 Pholus">5145 Pholus</a>, suggesting a similar region of origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sheppard2010_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sheppard2010-36">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>Trujillo and colleagues have placed upper limits in Sedna's surface composition of 60% for methane ice and 70% for water ice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo2005_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo2005-35">[30]</a></sup> The presence of methane further supports the existence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tholins" title="Tholins">tholins</a> on Sedna's surface, because they are produced by irradiation of methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emery2007_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Emery2007-37">[32]</a></sup> Barucci and colleagues compared Sedna's spectrum with that of <a href="/wiki/Triton_(moon)" title="Triton (moon)">Triton</a> and detected weak <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absorption_bands" title="Absorption bands">absorption bands</a> belonging to methane and <a href="/wiki/Solid_nitrogen" title="Solid nitrogen">nitrogen ices</a>. From these observations, they suggested the following model of the surface: 24% Triton-type <a href="/wiki/Tholin" title="Tholin">tholins</a>, 7% <a href="/wiki/Amorphous_carbon" title="Amorphous carbon">amorphous carbon</a>, 10% nitrogen, 26% <a href="/wiki/Methanol" title="Methanol">methanol</a>, and 33% methane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Triton_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Triton-38">[33]</a></sup> The detection of methane and water ices was confirmed in 2006 by the Spitzer Space Telescope <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-infrared" title="Mid-infrared">mid-infrared</a> photometry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emery2007_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Emery2007-37">[32]</a></sup> The presence of <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> on the surface suggests the possibility that, at least for a short time, Sedna may have a tenuous atmosphere. During a 200-year period near perihelion, the maximum temperature on Sedna should exceed 35.6 K (−237.6 °C), the transition temperature between alpha-phase solid N<sub>2</sub> and the beta phase seen on Triton. At 38 K, the N<sub>2</sub> <a href="/wiki/Vapor_pressure" title="Vapor pressure">vapor pressure</a> would be 14 microbar (1.4 Pa or 0.000014 atm).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Triton_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Triton-38">[33]</a></sup> However, its deep red <a href="/wiki/Spectral_slope" title="Spectral slope">spectral slope</a> is indicative of high concentrations of <a href="/wiki/Organic_chemistry" title="Organic chemistry">organic material</a> on its surface, and its weak methane absorption bands indicate that methane on Sedna's surface is ancient, rather than freshly deposited. This means that Sedna is too cold for methane to evaporate from its surface and then fall back as snow, which happens on Triton and probably on Pluto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Emery2007_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Emery2007-37">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>Models of internal heating via <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> suggest that Sedna might be capable of supporting a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subsurface_ocean" title="Subsurface ocean">subsurface ocean</a> of liquid water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hussman2006_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hussman2006-39">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In their paper announcing the discovery of Sedna, Mike Brown and his colleagues described it as the first observed body belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Oort_cloud" title="Oort cloud">Oort cloud</a>, the hypothetical cloud of <a href="/wiki/Comet" title="Comet">comets</a> thought to exist nearly a light-year from the Sun. They observed that, unlike <a href="/wiki/Scattered_disc" title="Scattered disc">scattered disc</a> objects such as <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, Sedna's perihelion (76 AU) is too distant for it to have been scattered by the gravitational influence of Neptune.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_15-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mike-15">[12]</a></sup> Because it is a great deal closer to the Sun than was expected for an Oort cloud object, and has an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inclination" title="Inclination">inclination</a> roughly in line with the planets and the Kuiper belt, they described the planetoid as being an "inner Oort cloud object", situated in the disc reaching from the Kuiper belt to the spherical part of the cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swiss_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-Swiss-40">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LykDyn_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-LykDyn-41">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>If Sedna formed in its current location, the Sun's original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Protoplanetary_disc" title="Protoplanetary disc">protoplanetary disc</a> must have extended as far as 75 AU into space.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SternAJ2005_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-SternAJ2005-42">[37]</a></sup> Also, Sedna's initial orbit must have been approximately circular, otherwise its formation by the <a href="/wiki/Accretion_(astrophysics)" title="Accretion (astrophysics)">accretion</a> of smaller bodies into a whole would not have been possible, because the large <a href="/wiki/Relative_velocity" title="Relative velocity">relative velocities</a> between planetesimals would have been too disruptive. Therefore, it must have been tugged into its current eccentric orbit by a gravitational interaction with another body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-scattered_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-scattered-43">[38]</a></sup> In their initial paper, Brown, Rabinowitz and colleagues suggested three possible candidates for the perturbing body: an unseen planet beyond the Kuiper belt, a single <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_nearest_stars#Future_and_past" title="List of nearest stars">passing star</a>, or one of the young stars embedded with the Sun in the stellar cluster in which it formed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_15-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mike-15">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Mike Brown and his team favored the hypothesis that Sedna was lifted into its current orbit by a star from the Sun's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Birth_cluster" title="Birth cluster">birth cluster</a>, arguing that Sedna's aphelion of about 1,000 AU, which is relatively close compared to those of long-period comets, is not distant enough to be affected by passing stars at their current distances from the Sun. They propose that Sedna's orbit is best explained by the Sun having formed in an <a href="/wiki/Open_cluster" title="Open cluster">open cluster</a> of several stars that gradually disassociated over time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_15-4"><a href="#cite_note-Mike-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown2004AAS205_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown2004AAS205-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanetarySociety_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlanetarySociety-45">[40]</a></sup> That hypothesis has also been advanced by both <a href="/wiki/Alessandro_Morbidelli_(astronomer)" title="Alessandro Morbidelli (astronomer)">Alessandro Morbidelli</a> and <a href="/wiki/Scott_Jay_Kenyon" title="Scott Jay Kenyon">Scott Jay Kenyon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2004_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2004-46">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenyon2004_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kenyon2004-47">[42]</a></sup> Computer simulations by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Julio_A._Fernandez" title="Julio A. Fernandez">Julio A. Fernandez</a> and Adrian Brunini suggest that multiple close passes by young stars in such a cluster would pull many objects into Sedna-like orbits.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_15-5"><a href="#cite_note-Mike-15">[12]</a></sup> A study by Morbidelli and <a href="/wiki/Harold_F._Levison" title="Harold F. Levison">Levison</a> suggested that the most likely explanation for Sedna's orbit was that it had been perturbed by a close (approximately 800 AU) pass by another star in the first 100 million years or so of the Solar System's existence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2004_46-1"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2004-46">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-challenge_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-challenge-48">[43]</a></sup></p>

<area alt="Orcus" coords="271,147,8" href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" shape="circle" title="Orcus"></area>
<area alt="Orcus" coords="259,176,284,182" href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" shape="rect" title="Orcus"></area>

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:EightTNOs.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artistic comparison of <a href="/wiki/Pluto" title="Pluto">Pluto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(dwarf_planet)" title="Eris (dwarf planet)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Makemake" title="Makemake">Makemake</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haumea" title="Haumea">Haumea</a>, <strong class="selflink">Sedna</strong>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>, <a href="/wiki/50000_Quaoar" title="50000 Quaoar">Quaoar</a>, <a href="/wiki/90482_Orcus" title="90482 Orcus">Orcus</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a> along with the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a>.
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini" style="float:right"><span style="margin-right:-0.125em">[</span>
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:TNO_imagemap" title="Template:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:TNO_imagemap" title="Template talk:TNO imagemap"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:TNO_imagemap&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
<span style="margin-left:-0.125em">]</span></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Neptunian_planet" title="Trans-Neptunian planet">trans-Neptunian planet</a> hypothesis has been advanced in several forms by a number of astronomers, including Rodney Gomes and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Patryk_Lykawka&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Patryk Lykawka (page does not exist)">Patryk Lykawka</a>. One scenario involves perturbations of Sedna's orbit by a hypothetical planetary-sized body in the Hills cloud. Recent simulations show that Sedna's orbital traits could be explained by perturbations by a Neptune-mass object at 2,000 AU (or less), a Jupiter-mass (<a href="/wiki/Jupiter_mass" title="Jupiter mass"><i>M</i><sub>J</sub></a>) at 5,000 AU, or even an Earth-mass object at 1,000 AU.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlanetarySociety_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-PlanetarySociety-45">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomez2006_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez2006-49">[44]</a></sup> Computer simulations by Patryk Lykawka have suggested that Sedna's orbit may have been caused by a body roughly the size of Earth, ejected outward by Neptune early in the Solar System's formation and currently in an elongated orbit between 80 and 170 AU from the Sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lykawka_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-lykawka-50">[45]</a></sup> Mike Brown's various sky surveys have not detected any Earth-sized objects out to a distance of about 100 AU. However, it is possible that such an object may have been scattered out of the Solar System after the formation of the inner Oort cloud.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sisters_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-sisters-51">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Caltech researchers <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Batygin" title="Konstantin Batygin">Konstantin Batygin</a> and Mike Brown have found evidence of a giant planet with a highly eccentric orbit in the outer Solar System. The object, which the researchers have nicknamed <a href="/wiki/Planet_Nine" title="Planet Nine">Planet Nine</a>, has a mass about 10 times that of Earth and orbits about 20 times farther from the Sun on average than does Neptune (which orbits the Sun at an average distance of 30.1 astronomical units (4.50<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>9</sup> km)). In fact, it would take this new planet between 10,000 and 20,000 years to make just one full orbit around the Sun. The researchers hypothesised the planet's existence through mathematical modeling and computer simulations, but have not yet observed the object directly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>It has been suggested that Sedna's orbit is the result of influence by a large binary companion to the Sun, thousands of AU distant. One such hypothetical companion is <a href="/wiki/Nemesis_(hypothetical_star)" title="Nemesis (hypothetical star)">Nemesis</a>, a dim <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binary_star_system" title="Binary star system">companion</a> to the Sun that has been proposed to be responsible for the supposed periodicity of <a href="/wiki/Extinction_event" title="Extinction event">mass extinctions</a> on Earth from cometary impacts, the lunar impact record, and the common orbital elements of a number of long-period comets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomez2006_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez2006-49">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cruttenden_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cruttenden-53">[48]</a></sup> However, to date no direct evidence of Nemesis has been found, and many lines of evidence (such as <a href="/wiki/Crater_counting" title="Crater counting">crater counts</a>), have thrown its existence into doubt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hills1984_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hills1984-54">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Planck-NemesisMyth_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Planck-NemesisMyth-55">[50]</a></sup> <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=John_J._Matese&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="John J. Matese (page does not exist)">John J. Matese</a> and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Daniel_P._Whitmire&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Daniel P. Whitmire (page does not exist)">Daniel P. Whitmire</a>, longtime proponents of the possibility of a wide binary companion to the Sun, have suggested that an object of <span class="nowrap">5</span> <i>M</i><sub>J</sub> lying at roughly 7,850 AU from the Sun could produce a body in Sedna's orbit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Matese2006_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Matese2006-56">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Morbidelli and <a href="/wiki/Scott_Jay_Kenyon" title="Scott Jay Kenyon">Kenyon</a> have also suggested that Sedna did not originate in the Solar System, but was captured by the Sun from a passing extrasolar <a href="/wiki/Planetary_system" title="Planetary system">planetary system</a>, specifically that of a <a href="/wiki/Brown_dwarf" title="Brown dwarf">brown dwarf</a> about 1/20th the <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">mass of the Sun</a> (<a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass"><i>M</i><sub>☉</sub></a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2004_46-2"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2004-46">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kenyon2004_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kenyon2004-47">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sednoid" title="Sednoid">Sednoid</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg"><img alt="the Sun appears merely as a point of light, distended by dust. The surface of Sedna is red ice, dimly glimmering in the noontime sunlight" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="2738" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg/220px-Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg/330px-Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg/440px-Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artist%27s_concept_of_the_Solar_System_as_viewed_from_Sedna.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Artist's conception of the surface of Sedna, with the <a href="/wiki/Milky_Way" title="Milky Way">Milky Way</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antares" title="Antares">Antares</a>, the Sun and <a href="/wiki/Spica" title="Spica">Spica</a> above</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Sedna's highly elliptical orbit means that the probability of its detection was roughly 1 in 80, which suggests that, unless its discovery was a fluke, another 40–120 Sedna-sized objects would exist within the same region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mike_15-6"><a href="#cite_note-Mike-15">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-largest_28-1"><a href="#cite_note-largest-28">[24]</a></sup> Another object, <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2000_CR105" title="2000 CR105">2000 CR<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">105</span></span></a></span>, has a similar but less extreme orbit: it has a perihelion of 44.3 AU, an aphelion of 394 AU, and an orbital period of 3,240 years. It may have been affected by the same processes as Sedna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Morbidelli2004_46-3"><a href="#cite_note-Morbidelli2004-46">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Each of the proposed mechanisms for Sedna's extreme orbit would leave a distinct mark on the structure and dynamics of any wider population. If a trans-Neptunian planet was responsible, all such objects would share roughly the same perihelion (about 80 AU). If Sedna were captured from another planetary system that rotated in the same direction as the Solar System, then all of its population would have orbits on relatively low inclinations and have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">semi-major axes</a> ranging from 100–500 AU. If it rotated in the opposite direction, then two populations would form, one with low and one with high inclinations. The perturbations from passing stars would produce a wide variety of perihelia and inclinations, each dependent on the number and angle of such encounters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sisters_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-sisters-51">[46]</a></sup></p>
<p>Acquiring a larger sample of such objects would help in determining which scenario is most likely.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwamb_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Schwamb-59">[54]</a></sup> "I call Sedna a fossil record of the earliest Solar System", said Brown in 2006. "Eventually, when other fossil records are found, Sedna will help tell us how the Sun formed and the number of stars that were close to the Sun when it formed."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fussman_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-fussman-16">[13]</a></sup> A 2007–2008 survey by Brown, Rabinowitz and Megan Schwamb attempted to locate another member of Sedna's hypothetical population. Although the survey was sensitive to movement out to 1,000 AU and discovered the likely dwarf planet <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>, it detected no new sednoid.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwamb_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Schwamb-59">[54]</a></sup> Subsequent simulations incorporating the new data suggested about 40 Sedna-sized objects probably exist in this region, with the brightest being about Eris's magnitude (−1.0).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwamb_59-2"><a href="#cite_note-Schwamb-59">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, astronomers announced the discovery of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2012_VP113" title="2012 VP113">2012 VP<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">113</span></span></a></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trujillo2014_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trujillo2014-23">[20]</a></sup> an object half the size of Sedna in a 4200-year orbit similar to Sedna's and a perihelion within Sedna's range of roughly 80 AU,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_2012_VP113_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata_2012_VP113-60">[55]</a></sup> which led some to speculate that it offered evidence of a trans-Neptunian planet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Physorg_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Physorg-61">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:502px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="942" data-file-width="1280" height="368" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png/500px-Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png/750px-Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png/1000px-Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png 2x" width="500"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Celestia_distant_object_orbits.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Sedna compared to some other very distant orbiting bodies</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Minor_Planet_Center" title="Minor Planet Center">Minor Planet Center</a>, which officially catalogs the objects in the Solar System, classifies Sedna as a scattered object.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MPC_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-MPC-62">[57]</a></sup> However, this grouping is heavily questioned, and many astronomers have suggested that it, together with a few other objects (e.g. <span class="nowrap"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2000_CR105" title="2000 CR105">2000 CR<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">105</span></span></a></span>), be placed in a new category of distant objects named <i>extended scattered disc objects</i> (E-SDO),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gladman_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gladman-63">[58]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Detached_object" title="Detached object">detached objects</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jewitt2006_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jewitt2006-64">[59]</a></sup> <i>distant detached objects</i> (DDO),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gomez2006_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-Gomez2006-49">[44]</a></sup> or <i>scattered-extended</i> in the formal classification by the <a href="/wiki/Deep_Ecliptic_Survey" title="Deep Ecliptic Survey">Deep Ecliptic Survey</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DES_Elliot2006_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-DES_Elliot2006-65">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>The discovery of Sedna resurrected the question of which <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_object" title="Astronomical object">astronomical objects</a> should be considered <a href="/wiki/Planet" title="Planet">planets</a> and which should not. On 15 March 2004, articles on Sedna in the popular press reported that a tenth planet had been discovered. This question was answered under the <a href="/wiki/International_Astronomical_Union" title="International Astronomical Union">International Astronomical Union</a> <a href="/wiki/IAU_definition_of_planet" title="IAU definition of planet">definition of a planet</a>, adopted on 24 August 2006, which mandated that a planet must have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cleared_the_neighborhood" title="Cleared the neighborhood">cleared the neighborhood</a> around its orbit. Sedna has a Stern–Levison parameter estimated to be much less than 1,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[f]</a></sup> and therefore cannot be considered to have cleared the neighborhood, even though no other objects have yet been discovered in its vicinity. To be a dwarf planet, Sedna must be in <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatic_equilibrium" title="Hydrostatic equilibrium">hydrostatic equilibrium</a>. It is bright enough, and therefore large enough, that this is expected to be the case,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-68">[62]</a></sup> and several astronomers have called it one.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tancredi2008_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tancredi2008-72">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brown-dplist_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brown-dplist-73">[67]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exploration">Exploration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Exploration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sedna will come to perihelion around 2075–2076.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-footnoteC_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-footnoteC-25">[d]</a></sup> This close approach to the Sun provides an opportunity for study that will not occur again for 12,000 years. Although Sedna is listed on NASA's Solar System exploration website,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASA1504_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASA1504-74">[68]</a></sup> NASA is not known to be considering any type of mission at this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NASADwarfMissions_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-NASADwarfMissions-75">[69]</a></sup> It was calculated that a flyby mission to Sedna could take 24.48 years using a Jupiter gravity assist, based on launch dates of 6 May 2033 and 23 June 2046. Sedna would be 77.27 or 76.43 AU from the Sun when the spacecraft arrives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McGranaghan_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-McGranaghan-76">[70]</a></sup></p>

</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-footnoteG-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteG_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteG_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Given the <a href="/wiki/Orbital_eccentricity" title="Orbital eccentricity">orbital eccentricity</a> of this object, different <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(astronomy)" title="Epoch (astronomy)">epochs</a> can generate quite different heliocentric unperturbed <a href="/wiki/Two-body_problem" title="Two-body problem">two-body</a> <a href="/wiki/Curve_fitting" title="Curve fitting">best-fit</a> solutions to the orbital period. Using a 1950 epoch, Sedna has a 12,100-year period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DES_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-DES-2">[2]</a></sup> but using a 2010 epoch Sedna has an 11,800-year period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-4">[4]</a></sup> For objects at such high eccentricity, the Sun's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barycentric_coordinates_(astronomy)" title="Barycentric coordinates (astronomy)">barycentric coordinates</a> are more stable than heliocentric coordinates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaib2009_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaib2009-5">[5]</a></sup> Using <a href="/wiki/JPL_Horizons_On-Line_Ephemeris_System" title="JPL Horizons On-Line Ephemeris System">JPL Horizons</a>, the barycentric orbital period is approximately 11,400 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barycenter_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-barycenter-3">[3]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-13"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-13">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">As of 2014<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, Sedna was about 86.3 <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">AU</a> from the Sun;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys-10">[9]</a></sup> Eris, the most massive known <a href="/wiki/Dwarf_planet" title="Dwarf planet">dwarf planet</a>, and <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span>, the largest object in the Solar System without a name, are currently farther from the Sun than Sedna at 96.4 AU and 87.0 AU, respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AstDys-Eris_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-AstDys-Eris-12">[11]</a></sup> Eris is near its aphelion (farthest distance from the Sun), whereas Sedna is nearing its 2076 <a href="/wiki/Perihelion_and_aphelion" title="Perihelion and aphelion">perihelion</a> (closest approach to the Sun).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons2076_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons2076-11">[10]</a></sup> Sedna will overtake Eris as the farthest known large object in the Solar System in 2114, but the <a href="/wiki/List_of_possible_dwarf_planets#Under_3" title="List of possible dwarf planets">probable dwarf planet</a> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(225088)_2007_OR10" title="(225088) 2007 OR10">2007 OR<sub>10</sub></a></span> has recently overtaken Sedna and will overtake Eris by 2045.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons2076_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons2076-11">[10]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-footnoteF-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteF_14-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteF_14-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Possible dwarf planet <a href="/wiki/2014_FE72" title="2014 FE72">2014 FE72</a> has a period of ~90,000 years, and <a href="/wiki/Small_Solar_System_body" title="Small Solar System body">amall Solar System bodies</a> such as <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(308933)_2006_SQ372" title="(308933) 2006 SQ372">(308933) 2006 SQ<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">372</span></span></a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2005_VX3" title="2005 VX3">2005 VX<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">3</span></span></a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/(87269)_2000_OO67" title="(87269) 2000 OO67">(87269) 2000 OO<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">67</span></span></a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2002_RN109" title="2002 RN109">2002 RN<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">109</span></span></a></span>, <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/2007_TG422" title="2007 TG422">2007 TG<span style="position: relative; top: 0.3em;"><span style="font-size:80%;">422</span></span></a></span>, and several comets (such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Comet_of_1577" title="Great Comet of 1577">Great Comet of 1577</a>) also have larger heliocentric orbits. Of the latter, only <span class="nowrap">(308933) 2006 SQ<sub>372</sub></span>, <span class="nowrap">(87269) 2000 OO<sub>67</sub></span>, and <span class="nowrap">2007 TG<sub>422</sub></span> have a perihelion point farther than Jupiter's orbit, so it is debatable whether or not most of these objects are misclassified comets.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-footnoteC-25"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteC_25-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-footnoteC_25-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">Different programs using different <a href="/wiki/Epoch_(astronomy)" title="Epoch (astronomy)">epochs</a> and/or <a href="/wiki/Data_set" title="Data set">data sets</a> will produce slightly different dates for Sedna's <a href="/wiki/Apsis" title="Apsis">perihelion</a>. Using a 2014 epoch, the <a href="/wiki/JPL_Small-Body_Database" title="JPL Small-Body Database">JPL Small-Body Database</a> has a perihelion date of 2076.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jpldata_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-jpldata-4">[4]</a></sup> Using a 1990 epoch the Lowell <a href="/wiki/Deep_Ecliptic_Survey" title="Deep Ecliptic Survey">DES</a> has perihelion on <a class="external text" href="http://www.boulder.swri.edu/~buie/kbo/astrom/90377.html" rel="nofollow">2479282.9591</a> <a class="external text" href="http://ssd.jpl.nasa.gov/tc.cgi" rel="nofollow">(2075-12-11)</a> As of 2010<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=90377_Sedna&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, the <a href="/wiki/JPL_Horizons_On-Line_Ephemeris_System" title="JPL Horizons On-Line Ephemeris System">JPL Horizons</a> (using <a href="/wiki/Numerical_integration" title="Numerical integration">numerical integration</a>) indicated a perihelion date of 2076-Jul-18.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Horizons2076_11-4"><a href="#cite_note-Horizons2076-11">[10]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-29">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The HST search found no satellite candidates to a limit of about 500 times fainter than Sedna (Brown and Suer 2007).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-largest_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-largest-28">[24]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-67"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-67">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The Stern–Levison parameter (<i>Λ</i>) as defined by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Stern" title="Alan Stern">Alan Stern</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harold_F._Levison" title="Harold F. Levison">Harold F. Levison</a> in 2002 determines if an object will eventually clear its orbital neighbourhood of small bodies. It is defined as the object's fraction of solar mass (i.e. the object's mass divided by the Sun's mass) squared, divided by its semi-major axis to the 3/2 power, times a constant 1.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">16</span></sup>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stern_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-stern-66">[61]</a></sup><sup>(see equation 4)</sup> If an object's Λ is greater than 1, then that object will eventually clear its neighbourhood, and it can be considered for planethood. Using the unlikely highest estimated mass for Sedna of 2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">21</span></sup> kg, Sedna's Λ is (2<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">21</span></sup>/<a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">1.9891<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">30</span></sup></a>)<sup>2</sup> / 519<sup>3/2</sup> × 1.7<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup><span style="white-space:nowrap">16</span></sup> = 1.44<span style="margin:0 .15em 0 .25em">×</span>10<sup>−<span style="white-space:nowrap">6</span></sup>. This is much less than 1, so Sedna is not a planet by this criterion.</span></li>
</ol>
</div>
